{"uri": "2024-07-409261677", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-02", "time": "21:00:38", "dateTime": "2024-07-02T21:00:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-02T15:12:45Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9843137264251709, "url": "https://www.aol.com/news/ai-learning-said-reddit-stack-151245574.html", "title": "AI is learning from what you said on Reddit, Stack Overflow or Facebook. Are you OK with that?", "body": "AI is learning from what you said on Reddit, Stack Overflow or Facebook. Are you OK with that?\n\nMATT O'BRIEN\n\nJuly 2, 2024 at 8:46 PM\n\nCAMBRIDGE, Mass. (AP) -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\n\"Keeping contributors incentivized to provide commentary should be paramount,\" he said.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said in an interview. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought. And we're one of those places.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\n\"The vast majority of people just have no idea that their data is being used,\" Gilbert said.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nReddit hasn't tried to punish users who protested -- nor could it easily do so given how much say voluntary moderators have on what happens in their specialty forums known as subreddits. But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don't want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There's apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. But historically Reddit has been for connecting with humans.\"\n\nShe said it's ironic that the AI-generated content threatening Reddit was sourced on the comments of millions of human Redditors, and \"there's a real risk that eventually it could end up pushing people out.\"\n\n-- --\n\nAssociated Press writer El\u00e9onore Hughes in Rio de Janeiro contributed to this report.\n\n-- --\n\nThe Associated Press and OpenAI have a licensing and technology agreement that allows OpenAI access to part of AP's text archives.", "source": {"uri": "aol.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Aol"}, {"uri": "8206287883", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-02", "time": "15:24:20", "dateTime": "2024-07-02T15:24:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-02T15:23:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9803921580314636, "url": "https://www.independent.co.uk/news/ap-reddit-people-gilbert-sarah-gilbert-b2572668.html", "title": "AI is learning from what you said on Reddit, Stack Overflow or Facebook. Are you OK with that?", "body": "Our mission is to deliver unbiased, fact-based reporting that holds power to account and exposes the truth.\n\nWhether $5 or $50, every contribution counts.\n\nSupport us to deliver journalism without an agenda.\n\nPost a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\n\"Keeping contributors incentivized to provide commentary should be paramount,\" he said.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said in an interview. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought. And we're one of those places.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\n\"The vast majority of people just have no idea that their data is being used,\" Gilbert said.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nReddit hasn't tried to punish users who protested -- nor could it easily do so given how much say voluntary moderators have on what happens in their specialty forums known as subreddits. But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don't want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There's apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. But historically Reddit has been for connecting with humans.\"\n\nShe said it's ironic that the AI-generated content threatening Reddit was sourced on the comments of millions of human Redditors, and \"there's a real risk that eventually it could end up pushing people out.\"\n\n-- --\n\nAssociated Press writer El\u00e9onore Hughes in Rio de Janeiro contributed to this report.\n\n-- --\n\nThe A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\n\"Keeping contributors incentivized to provide commentary should be paramount,\" he said.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said in an interview. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought. And we're one of those places.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\n\"The vast majority of people just have no idea that their data is being used,\" Gilbert said.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nReddit hasn't tried to punish users who protested -- nor could it easily do so given how much say voluntary moderators have on what happens in their specialty forums known as subreddits. But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don't want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There's apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. But historically Reddit has been for connecting with humans.\"\n\nShe said it's ironic that the AI-generated content threatening Reddit was sourced on the comments of millions of human Redditors, and \"there's a real risk that eventually it could end up pushing people out.\"\n\n-- --\n\nAssociated Press writer El\u00e9onore Hughes in Rio de Janeiro contributed to this report.\n\n-- --\n\nThe Associated Press and OpenAI have a licensing and technology agreement that allows OpenAI access to part of AP's text archives.", "source": {"uri": "the-journal.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? And we\u00b4re one of those places.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives - or doesn't - after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility - which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot - some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow - and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people\u00b4s public posts into training AI systems - which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\n\"The vast majority of people just have no idea that their data is being used,\" Gilbert said.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach - partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform\u00b4s approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nReddit hasn't tried to punish users who protested - nor could it easily do so given how much say voluntary moderators have on what happens in their specialty forums known as subreddits. But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don\u00b4t want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There\u00b4s apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. But historically Reddit has been for connecting with humans.\"\n\nShe said it's ironic that the AI-generated content threatening Reddit was sourced on the comments of millions of human Redditors, and \"there\u00b4s a real risk that eventually it could end up pushing people out.\"\n\n--\n\nAssociated Press writer El\u00e9onore Hughes in Rio de Janeiro contributed to this report.\n\n--\n\nThe {"uri": "8209637460", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-04", "time": "13:37:21", "dateTime": "2024-07-04T13:37:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-04T13:37:02Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9686274528503418, "url": "https://www.nwitimes.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/genai-training-data-stack-overflow-reddit-facebook/article_b92c9b6c-3a28-5ce3-902a-bdc86b1f5b0f.html", "title": "AI is learning from what you said on Reddit, Stack Overflow or Facebook. Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don't want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There's apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\n\"Keeping contributors incentivized to provide commentary should be paramount,\" he said.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said in an interview. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought. And we're one of those places.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\n\"The vast majority of people just have no idea that their data is being used,\" Gilbert said.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nReddit hasn't tried to punish users who protested -- nor could it easily do so given how much say voluntary moderators have on what happens in their specialty forums known as subreddits. But what worries Gilbert, who helps moderate the \"AskHistorians\" subreddit, is the increasing flow of AI-generated commentary that moderators must decide whether to allow or ban.\n\n\"People come to Reddit because they want to talk to people, they don't want to talk to bots,\" Gilbert said. \"There's apps where they can talk to bots if they want to. But historically Reddit has been for connecting with humans.\"\n\nShe said it's ironic that the AI-generated content threatening Reddit was sourced on the comments of millions of human Redditors, and \"there's a real risk that eventually it could end up pushing people out.\"\n\n-- --\n\nAssociated Press writer El\u00e9onore Hughes in Rio de Janeiro contributed to this report.\n\n-- --\n\nThe Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... McLean County correctional officer fired, charged with sexual misconduct involving inmate Flick Fact: How these three Illinois brothers became mega-millionaires? Departing Bloomington-Normal priests look to past, future Crystal Lake man identified in fatal crash near Downs Bloomington man pleads guilty to battering an officer Dr. Ashvin K. Patel Flick Fact: Besides Chicago, what other Illinois city once had an NBA franchise? Rivian deal with Volkswagen raises prospects for Georgia plant Flick Fact: What company employs so many in Illinois, it'd be one of our largest cities? Ag-focused tech hub in 'Silicorn Valley' Central Illinois awarded $51M federal grant Bloomington nonprofit at risk of relocation without new roof Make Music Normal finds the beat for 10th year uptown Heartland Community College announces new head cross country coach Aaron Rossi sentenced to 5 years in prison on federal fraud case Supreme Court declines to hear Illinois assault weapons ban challenge\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... North Platte School Board continues debate on teacher dress code Man taken into custody near Brady after pursuit, manhunt Saturday Kayaker says Nebraska a 'water wonderland' filled with hidden gems Missouri church announcement called for young men to form local militia. It was a mistake, pastor says K9 unit used to track, arrest man near Brady after Saturday pursuit, manhunt More businesses inevitable on road to Maloney, Planning Commission tells boat-shop foes Hispanic residents account for over 80% of Nebraska population growth 2024 British Open odds: Favorites, long shot picks for Open Championship at Royal Troon Crete, Nebraska: 7 shot in 'welcoming community' by neighbor who 'hated everybody' Perkins, the aging diner chain, has a 'new vibe' with a different name Softball-sized hail reported in Dawson County in severe thunderstorm early Saturday Martin Mull, hip comic and actor from 'Fernwood Tonight' and 'Roseanne,' dies at 80 Retired Paxton village clerk pursues her 'passion' with City Bakery 'Bigger, bolder, stronger': Opportunity Center begins rebuilding after tree falls on facility Annual moisture halfway to North Platte's 150-year Top 10\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... Atlantic City Beer and Music Festival canceled Nine rescued from waters off Ninth Street beach in Ocean City Wildwood: No more 'dog days' on the Boardwalk this summer Former site for Sister Jean's, 'Nucky' Johnson wedding to be demolished to make way for cannabis business Chicken Guy! restaurant debuts Tuesday in Atlantic City Water main break repaired in Wildwood Ousted Atlantic City school board member sues, alleging retaliation, racial discrimination Cape May Carriage horse dies on the job Your guide to Fourth of July fireworks in South Jersey Rain can't keep thousands away from Wildwood beach soccer tournament Galloway woman charged with 43 counts of neglect after multiple dead animals found at home 17-year-old dies in fatal Dennis Township crash Man found dead on Wildwood Crest beach NBA star Jalen Brunson, rapper Flavor Flav spotted in Atlantic City After an influential career, Mike Gatley steps down as Mainland Regional athletic director\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... North Lincoln grain elevator will soon come down after it was purchased at auction Crete, Nebraska: 7 shot in 'welcoming community' by neighbor who 'hated everybody' Lincoln in flood warning, more severe storms possible Tuesday afternoon 6 people injured in Crete shooting; suspect dead, State Patrol says 74-year-old identified as gunman in Crete shooting that injured 7 DJ Diesel, aka Shaq, draws superstar-sized crowd to Lincoln nightclub 'Let us in' -- Concertgoers voice frustrations after large crowd packed Royal Grove to see Shaq Baker's Candies store in Greenwood to double in size with multimillion-dollar expansion America's first official 'quiet trail' sits in the Nebraska Sandhills Lincoln's wind turbines blown up in northeast Lincoln Lincoln's Uncle Sam Jam celebration will now include food vendors Drivers will face nighttime detours on I-80 west of Lincoln this week Garner Industries relocates in Lincoln after tornado destroys plant Heavy rain causes flash flooding in Lincoln Omaha Supernovas sign 3 former Nebraska volleyball players\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie 66.6% believe AI could do their job Can AI be a stress reliever?
