Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... Foxglove Pub in Hamburg closes its doors: 'The business just wasn't for us' A Buffalo teacher thought he chatted with a teen online. He got a surprise when he went to meet her Mike Harrington: Sabres are (naturally) thrilled with another first-rounder, but what about the deals? Inside Clarence's new million-dollar home development Sabres acquire fourth-line winger Beck Malenstyn for second-round draft pick Mike Harrington: Kevyn Adams maintains offseason is 'incomplete.' So what happens after Skinner buyout? Erie County real estate transactions Lisa Krueger brings empathy, determination to West Seneca: 'I will not make everyone happy' Sabres begin process to walk away from Jeff Skinner's contract with three years left At fraud trial, Buffalo debt collectors describe their scripts threatening arrests: 'We told debtors we were coming' Buffalo State has dismissed its women's wrestling staff before its inaugural season. Here's what we know Niagara Falls firm sues Hotel Niagara developer for breach of contract July Fourth festivities in Western New York: Fireworks, music and food Buffalo Diocese's lawyers want $150,000 for legal work to keep sex abuse records secret Hamburg resident dies in crash on Niagara Thruway in Buffalo\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? {"uri": "8209645272", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-04", "time": "13:43:01", "dateTime": "2024-07-04T13:43:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-04T13:42:37Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://theeagle.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/genai-training-data-stack-overflow-reddit-facebook/article_8d3ee900-d55e-542e-a9e9-e2bfb0d950d1.html", "title": "AI is learning from what you said on Reddit, Stack Overflow or Facebook. Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... Texas A&M hires Washington head baseball coach Jason Kelly as pitching coach Even without college coaches, Texas A&M baseball camps must go on Texas A&M AD Trev Alberts details days leading up to Jim Schlossnagle's departure Watch: Jace LaViolette explains decision to stay at Texas A&M Bryan child dies in lightning strike, family starts GoFundMe Texas A&M hires Michael Earley as head baseball coach Bryan family settles into new house from Habitat for Humanity 'This is my home': Jace LaViolette, Gavin Grahovac announce they will return to Texas A&M Cessna: Scorned Aggies need to make Schlossnagle regret his move New Texas A&M baseball coach Michael Earley can teach hitting, but relationships is where he thrives Texas A&M baseball team set to compete in 2025 Astros Foundation College Classic Texas A&M Chancellor John Sharp announces he will retire in a year Texas A&M baseball team announces annual team awards Waco man arrested in Brazos County for alleged theft of a firearm Texas A&M infielder Kaeden Kent to return to Aggie baseball program\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... Missoula business owner issued sexual assault citation Missoula man presumed to have drowned on Flathead Lake Business Buzz: New eatery/bar, new acai bowl cafe, new flights from Missoula UPDATED: Officials ID teen killed in Lake County crash 'Devastating': Montana's Flathead cherry crop wiped out by January Arctic freeze Human remains found in Missoula County University of Montana researcher admits to federal fraud Person caught on camera tearing down another Pride flag from Missoula business UPDATED: Small grass fire burns 2 acres in Missoula North Hills Business Buzz: New arts center and 'cacao bar'; new splash pad In major homelessness case, US Supreme Court upholds ban on sleeping outside Billings' Christina Aragon advances to semifinals of 1,500 at Olympic Trials Eagle Watch Estates tenants protest under watch of HUD Montana to get $41M for Headwaters Tech Hub, according to Tester and Daines One airman killed, 5 injured in incident at Malmstrom Air Force Base, officials say\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? Are you OK with that?", "body": "CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Post a comment on Reddit, answer coding questions on Stack Overflow, edit a Wikipedia entry or share a baby photo on your public Facebook or Instagram feed and you are also helping to train the next generation of artificial intelligence.\n\nNot everyone is OK with that -- especially as the same online forums where they've spent years contributing are increasingly flooded with AI-generated commentary mimicking what real humans might say.\n\nSome longtime users have tried to delete their past contributions or rewrite them into gibberish, but the protests haven't had much effect. A handful of governments -- including Brazil's privacy regulator on Tuesday -- have also tried to step in.\n\nPeople are also reading... A rare classic car museum is expanding at a new Tucson location Rise in drivers behaving badly across metro Tucson grabs police attention Iconic Tucson bar expanding to Phoenix Don't sleep on the carne asada from The Quesadillas, which might be one of Tucson's best-kept secrets Monsoon is here! 9 Tucson restaurants and bars to watch the weather Arizona, Tucson actions on homeless may follow Supreme Court ruling Tornado touches down in Tucson, officials confirm Arizona firm named in Justice Department's $2.7B health care fraud crackdown Where to watch fireworks in Tucson this Fourth of July Take your pick from these Tucson-area Fourth of July fireworks shows 35 fun events happening in Tucson this weekend June 27-30 \ud83c\udf9e\ud83d\udc10 34 fun events happening in Tucson this weekend ESPN projects four Arizona Wildcats to be taken in 2025 NBA Draft Guiding a modern-day basketball 'Dream Team,' Steve Kerr aims to avoid a nightmare at Paris Olympics Tucson police: Siblings dead in apparent murder-suicide\n\n\"A more significant portion of the population just kind of feels helpless,\" said Reddit volunteer moderator Sarah Gilbert, who also studies online communities at Cornell University. \"There's nowhere to go except just completely going offline or not contributing in ways that bring value to them and value to others.\"\n\nPlatforms are responding -- with mixed results. Take Stack Overflow, the popular hub for computer programming tips. First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI at work: The impact of artificial intelligence in the workplace AI concerns increase with age Top concerns result in a tie Can AI be a stress reliever? First, it banned ChatGPT-written responses due to frequent errors, but now it's partnering with AI chatbot developers and has punished some of its own users who tried to erase their past contributions in protest.\n\nIt's one of a number of social media platforms grappling with user wariness -- and occasional revolts -- as they try to adapt to the changes brought by generative AI.\n\nSoftware developer Andy Rotering of Bloomington, Minnesota, has used Stack Overflow daily for 15 years and said he worries the company \"could be inadvertently hurting its greatest resource\" -- the community of contributors who've donated time to help other programmers.\n\nStack Overflow CEO Prashanth Chandrasekar said the company is trying to balance rising demand for instant chatbot-generated coding assistance with the desire for a community \"knowledge base\" where people still want to post and \"get recognized\" for what they've contributed.\n\n\"Fast forward five years -- there's going to be all sorts of machine-generated content on the web,\" he said. \"There's going to be very few places where there's truly authentic, original human thought.\"\n\nChandrasekar readily describes Stack Overflow's challenges as like one of the \"case studies\" he learned about at Harvard Business School, of a how a business survives -- or doesn't -- after a disruptive technological change.\n\nFor more than a decade, users typically landed on Stack Overflow after typing a coding question in Google, and then found the answer, copied and pasted it. The answers they were most likely to see came from volunteers who'd built up points measuring their credibility -- which in some cases could help land them a job.\n\nNow programmers can simply ask an AI chatbot -- some of which are already trained on everything ever posted to Stack Overflow -- and it can instantly spit out an answer.\n\nChatGPT's debut in late 2022 threatened to put Stack Overflow out of business. So Chandrasekar carved out a special 40-person team at the company to race out the launch of its own specialized AI chatbot, called Overflow AI. Then, the company made deals with Google and ChatGPT maker OpenAI, enabling the AI developers to tap into Stack Overflow's question-and-answer archive to further improve their AI large language models.\n\nThat kind of strategy makes sense but may have come too late, said Maria Roche, an assistant professor at Harvard Business School. \"I'm surprised that Stack Overflow wasn't working on this earlier,\" she said.\n\nWhen some Stack Overflow users tried to delete their past comments after the Open AI partnership was announced, the company responded by suspending their accounts due to terms that make all contributions \"perpetually and irrevocably licensed to Stack Overflow.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\n\"We quickly addressed it and said, 'Look, that's not acceptable behavior',\" said Chandrasekar, describing the protesters as a small minority in the \"low hundreds\" of the platform's 100 million users.\n\nBrazil's national data protection authority on Tuesday took action to ban social media giant Meta Platforms from training its AI models on the Facebook and Instagram posts of Brazilians. It established a daily fine of 50,000 reais ($8,820) for non-compliance.\n\nMeta in a statement called it a \"step backwards for innovation\" and said it has been more transparent than many industry counterparts doing similar AI training on public content, and that its practices comply with Brazilian laws.\n\nMeta has also encountered resistance in Europe, where it recently put on hold its plans to start feeding people's public posts into training AI systems -- which was supposed to start last week. In the U.S., where there's no national law protecting online privacy, such training is already likely happening.\n\nReddit has taken a different approach -- partnering with AI developers like OpenAI and Google while also making clear that content can't be taken in bulk without the platform's approval by commercial entities \"with no regard for user rights or privacy.\" The deals helped bring Reddit the money it needed to debut on Wall Street in March, with investors pushing the value of the company close to $9 billion seconds after it began trading on the New York Stock Exchange.\n\nAI at
